class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border: 1px solid #FFBF00;text-align:center;color:black;">Related topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-top:0;"><div class="plainlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Democratic_capitalism" title="Democratic capitalism">Democratic capitalism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Political_views_of_American_academics" title="Political views of American academics">Liberal bias in academia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Regressive_left" title="Regressive left">Regressive left</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Yellow_flag_waving.svg" class="image"><img alt="Yellow flag waving.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/15px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/22px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/30px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="268" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Liberalism" title="Portal:Liberalism">Liberalism&#32;portal</a></li>\n<li><span style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg" class="image"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics&#32;portal</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Liberalism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Liberal Party</b> was one of the two <a href="/wiki/Major_party" title="Major party">major</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Political parties in the United Kingdom">parties in the United Kingdom</a> \xe2\x80\x93 with the opposing <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> \xe2\x80\x93 in the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-BanchoffSmith1999_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BanchoffSmith1999-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It arose from an alliance of <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> <a href="/wiki/Peelite" title="Peelite">Peelites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radicals_(UK)" title="Radicals (UK)">Radicals</a> favourable to the ideals of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolutions</a> in the 1850s. By the end of the nineteenth century, it had formed four governments under <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Gladstone</a>. Despite being divided over the issue of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule Movement">Irish Home Rule</a>, the party returned to government in 1905 and then won <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1906" title="United Kingdom general election, 1906">a landslide victory</a> in the following year\'s general election.\n</p><p>Under <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Ministers</a> <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a> (1905-08) and <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> (1908-16), the Liberal Party passed the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_welfare_reforms" title="Liberal welfare reforms">welfare reforms</a> that created a basic British <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>. Although Asquith was <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Liberal Party (UK)">the party\'s leader</a>, its dominant figure was <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>. Asquith was overwhelmed by the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">wartime</a> role of <a href="/wiki/Asquith_coalition_ministry" title="Asquith coalition ministry">coalition</a> Prime Minister and Lloyd George replaced him as Prime Minister in late 1916, but Asquith remained as Liberal Party leader. The pair fought for years over control of the party, badly weakening it in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Martin_Pugh_(author)" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Pugh (author)">Martin Pugh</a> in <i>The Oxford Companion to British History</i> argues:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Lloyd George made a greater impact on British public life than any other 20th-century leader, thanks to his pre-war introduction of Britain\'s social welfare system (especially medical insurance, unemployment insurance, and old-age pensions, largely paid for by taxes on high incomes and on the land). Furthermore, in foreign affairs, he played a leading role in winning the First World War, redrawing the map of Europe at the peace conference, and partitioning Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_George_ministry" title="Lloyd George ministry">government of Lloyd George</a> was dominated by the Conservative Party, which finally deposed him in 1922. By the end of the 1920s, the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> had replaced the Liberals as the Conservatives\' main rival. The party went into decline after 1918 and by the 1950s won no more than six seats at general elections. Apart from notable <a href="/wiki/By-election" title="By-election">by-election</a> victories, the party\'s fortunes did not improve significantly until it formed the <a href="/wiki/SDP%E2%80%93Liberal_Alliance" title="SDP\xe2\x80\x93Liberal Alliance">SDP\xe2\x80\x93Liberal Alliance</a> with the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a> (SDP) in 1981. At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">1983 general election</a>, the Alliance won over a quarter of the vote, but only 23 of the 650 seats it contested. At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987 general election</a>, its share of the vote fell below 23% and the Liberal and Social Democratic parties merged in 1988 to form the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>. A splinter group reconstituted the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK,_1989)" title="Liberal Party (UK, 1989)">Liberal Party</a> in 1989. It was formed by party members opposed to the merger who saw the Liberal Democrats diluting Liberal ideals.\n</p><p>Prominent intellectuals associated with the Liberal Party include the philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, the economist <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> and social planner <a href="/wiki/William_Beveridge" title="William Beveridge">William Beveridge</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" coalition</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#The_splits_over_the_National_Government"><span class="tocnumber">1.9</span> <span class="toctext">The splits over the National 1859\xe2\x80\x931988</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Liberal_Leaders_in_the_House_of_Commons:_1859\xe2\x80\x931916"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Electoral performance</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palmerston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/Palmerston.jpg" width="190" height="286" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="190" data-file-height="286" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palmerston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Viscount_Palmerston" title="Viscount Palmerston">Viscount Palmerston</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Liberal Party grew out of the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a>, who had their origins in an <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocratic</a> faction in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> and the early 19th century <a href="/wiki/Radicals_(UK)" title="Radicals (UK)">Radicals</a>. The Whigs were in favour of reducing the power of the Crown and increasing the power of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament</a>. Although their motives in this were originally to gain more power for themselves, the more idealistic Whigs gradually came to support an expansion of <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> for its own sake. The great figures of reformist <a href="/wiki/Whiggism" title="Whiggism">Whiggery</a> were <a href="/wiki/Charles_James_Fox" title="Charles James Fox">Charles James Fox</a> (died 1806) and his disciple and successor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Earl Grey</a>. After decades in opposition, the Whigs returned to power under Grey in 1830 and carried the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">First Reform Act</a> in 1832.\n</p><p>The Reform Act was the climax of Whiggism, but it also brought about the Whigs\' demise. The admission of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle classes</a> to the franchise and to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> led eventually to the development of a systematic middle class liberalism and the end of Whiggery, although for many years reforming aristocrats held senior positions in the party. In the years after Grey\'s retirement, the party was led first by <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb,_2nd_Viscount_Melbourne" title="William Lamb, 2nd Viscount Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a>, a fairly traditional Whig, and then by <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Lord John Russell</a>, the son of a Duke but a crusading radical, and by <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry John Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a>, a renegade Irish <a href="/wiki/Tory" title="Tory">Tory</a> and essentially a <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservative</a>, although capable of radical gestures.\n</p><p>As early as 1839, Russell had adopted the name of "Liberals", but in reality his party was a loose coalition of Whigs in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> and Radicals in the Commons. The leading Radicals were <a href="/wiki/John_Bright" title="John Bright">John Bright</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cobden" title="Richard Cobden">Richard Cobden</a>, who represented the manufacturing towns which had gained representation under the Reform Act. They favoured social reform, personal liberty, reducing the powers of the Crown and the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> (many Liberals were <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">Nonconformists</a>), avoidance of war and foreign alliances (which were bad for business) and above all <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>. For a century, free trade remained the one cause which could unite all Liberals.\n</p><p>In 1841 the Liberals lost office to the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Sir Robert Peel</a>, but their period in <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Official Opposition (United Kingdom)">opposition</a> was short because the Conservatives split over the repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>, a free trade issue; and a faction known as the <a href="/wiki/Peelite" title="Peelite">Peelites</a> (but not Peel himself, who died soon after) defected to the Liberal side. This allowed ministries led by Russell, Palmerston and the Peelite <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton-Gordon,_4th_Earl_of_Aberdeen" title="George Hamilton-Gordon, 4th Earl of Aberdeen">Lord Aberdeen</a> to hold office for most of the 1850s and 1860s. A leading Peelite was <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>, who was a reforming <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> in most of these governments. The formal foundation of the Liberal Party is traditionally traced to 1859 and the formation of Palmerston\'s second government.\n</p><p>However, the Whig-Radical amalgam could not become a true modern political party while it was dominated by aristocrats and it was not until the departure of the "Two Terrible Old Men", Russell and Palmerston, that Gladstone could become the first leader of the modern Liberal Party. This was brought about by Palmerston\'s death in 1865 and Russell\'s retirement in 1868. After a brief Conservative government (during which the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1867" title="Reform Act 1867">Second Reform Act</a> was passed by agreement between the parties), Gladstone won a huge victory at the 1868 election and formed the first Liberal government. The establishment of the party as a national membership organisation came with the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberal_Federation" title="National Liberal Federation">National Liberal Federation</a> in 1877. <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> was also a Liberal MP from 1865 to 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Gladstonian_era">The Gladstonian era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The Gladstonian era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acgladstone2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Acgladstone2.jpg/220px-Acgladstone2.jpg" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Acgladstone2.jpg/330px-Acgladstone2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Acgladstone2.jpg/440px-Acgladstone2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="590" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acgladstone2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a></div></div></div>\n<p>For the next thirty years Gladstone and Liberalism were synonymous. <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> served as prime minister four times (1868\xe2\x80\x9374, 1880\xe2\x80\x9385, 1886, and 1892\xe2\x80\x9394). His financial policies, based on the notion of balanced budgets, low taxes and <i><a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a></i>, were suited to a developing capitalist society, but they could not respond effectively as economic and social conditions changed. Called the "Grand Old Man" later in life, Gladstone was always a dynamic popular orator who appealed strongly to the <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> and to the lower middle class. Deeply religious, Gladstone brought a new moral tone to politics, with his evangelical sensibility and his opposition to aristocracy.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His moralism often angered his upper-class opponents (including Queen Victoria), and his heavy-handed control split the Liberal Party.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In foreign policy, Gladstone was in general against foreign entanglements, but he did not resist the realities of imperialism. For example, he ordered the occupation of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt_under_the_British" title="History of Egypt under the British">Egypt</a> by British forces in 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  His goal was to create a European order based on co-operation rather than conflict and on mutual trust instead of rivalry and suspicion; the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> was to supplant the reign of force and self-interest. This Gladstonian concept of a harmonious <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Concert of Europe</a> was opposed to and ultimately defeated by a <a href="/wiki/Bismarckian" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismarckian">Bismarckian</a> system of manipulated alliances and antagonisms.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As prime minister 1868 to 1874, Gladstone headed a Liberal Party which was a coalition of Peelites like himself, Whigs and Radicals \xe2\x80\x93 he was now a spokesman for "peace, economy and reform." One major achievement was the <a href="/wiki/Elementary_Education_Act_1870" title="Elementary Education Act 1870">Elementary Education Act of 1870</a>, which provided England with an adequate system of elementary schools for the first time. He also secured the abolition of the purchase of commissions in the army and of religious tests for admission to Oxford and Cambridge; the introduction of the secret ballot in elections; the legalization of trade unions; and the reorganization of the judiciary in the Judicature Act.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Regarding Ireland, the major Liberal achievements were land reform, where he <a href="/wiki/Irish_Land_Acts" title="Irish Land Acts">ended centuries of landlord oppression</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/State_religion#Disestablishment" title="State religion">disestablishment</a> of the (Anglican) <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Church_Act_1869" title="Irish Church Act 1869">Irish Church Act 1869</a>.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1874" title="United Kingdom general election, 1874">1874 general election</a> Gladstone was defeated by the Conservatives under <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Disraeli</a> during a sharp economic recession. He formally resigned as Liberal leader and was succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Compton_Cavendish,_8th_Duke_of_Devonshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Spencer Compton Cavendish, 8th Duke of Devonshire">Marquess of Hartington</a>, but he soon changed his mind and returned to active politics. He strongly disagreed with Disraeli\'s pro-<a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> foreign policy and in 1880 he conducted the first outdoor mass-election campaign in Britain, known as the <a href="/wiki/Midlothian_campaign" title="Midlothian campaign">Midlothian campaign</a>. The Liberals won a large majority in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1880" title="United Kingdom general election, 1880">1880 election</a>. Hartington ceded his place and Gladstone resumed office.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland_and_Home_Rule">Ireland and Home Rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ireland and Home Rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Among the consequences of the <a href="/wiki/Third_Reform_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Reform Act">Third Reform Act</a> (1884) was the giving of the vote to many Catholics in Ireland. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1885" title="United Kingdom general election, 1885">1885 general election</a> the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> held the balance of power in the House of Commons, and demanded <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a> as the price of support for a continued Gladstone ministry. Gladstone personally supported Home Rule, but a strong <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal Unionist">Liberal Unionist</a> faction led by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a>, along with the last of the Whigs, Hartington, opposed it. The Irish Home Rule bill gave all owners of Irish land a chance to sell to the state at a price equal to 20 years\' purchase of the rents and allowing tenants to purchase the land. Irish nationalist reaction was mixed, Unionist opinion was hostile, and the election addresses during the 1886 election revealed English radicals to be against the bill also. Among the Liberal rank and file, several Gladstonian candidates disowned the bill, reflecting fears at the constituency level that the interests of the working people were being sacrificed to finance a rescue operation for the landed elite.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The result was a catastrophic split in the Liberal Party, and heavy defeat in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1886" title="United Kingdom general election, 1886">1886 election</a> at the hands of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>. There was a final weak Gladstone ministry in 1892, but it also was dependent on Irish support and failed to get Irish Home Rule through the House of Lords.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Newcastle_Programme">The Newcastle Programme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The Newcastle Programme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Newcastle_Programme" title="The Newcastle Programme">The Newcastle Programme</a></div>\n<p>Historically, the aristocracy was divided between Conservatives and Liberals. However, when Gladstone committed to home rule for Ireland, Britain\'s upper classes largely abandoned the Liberal party, giving the Conservatives a large permanent majority in the House of Lords. High Society in London, following the Queen, largely ostracized home rulers, and Liberal clubs were badly split.  <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> took a major element of upper-class supporters out of the Party and into a third party called "Liberal Unionism" on the Irish issue. It collaborated with and eventually merged into the Conservative party.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  The Gladstonian liberals in 1891 adopted "<a href="/wiki/The_Newcastle_Programme" title="The Newcastle Programme">The Newcastle Programme</a> that included home rule for Ireland,  disestablishment of the Church of England in Wales and Scotland, tighter controls on the sale of liquor,  major extension of factory regulation, and various democratic political reforms. The Programme had a strong appeal to the Nonconformist middle-class Liberal element, which felt liberated by the departure of the aristocracy.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_trade_unions">Relations with trade unions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Relations with trade unions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A major long-term consequence of the Third Reform Act was the rise of <a href="/wiki/Liberal-Labour_(UK)" title="Liberal-Labour (UK)">Lib-Lab</a> candidates, in the absence of any committed <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>. The Act split all county constituencies (which were represented by multiple MPs) into single-member constituencies, roughly corresponding to population patterns. In areas with working class majorities, in particular coal-mining areas, Lib-Lab candidates were popular, and they received sponsorship and endorsement from <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a>. In the first election after the Act was passed (1885), thirteen were elected, up from two in 1874. The Third Reform Act also facilitated the demise of the Whig old guard: in two-member constituencies, it was common to pair a Whig and a radical under the Liberal banner. After the Third Reform Act, fewer Whigs were selected as candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform_policies">Reform policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reform policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A broad range of interventionist reforms were introduced by the 1892\xe2\x80\x9395 Liberal government. Amongst other measures, standards of accommodation and of teaching in schools were improved, factory inspection was made more stringent, and ministers used their powers to increase the wages and reduce the working hours of large numbers of male workers employed by the state.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Historian Walter L. Arnstein concludes:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Notable as the Gladstonian reforms had been, they had almost all remained within the nineteenth-century Liberal tradition of gradually removing the religious, economic, and political barriers that prevented men of varied creeds and classes from exercising their individual talents in order to improve themselves and their society. As the third quarter of the century drew to a close, the essential bastions of Victorianism still held firm: respectability; a government of aristocrats and gentlemen now influenced not only by middle-class merchants and manufacturers but also by industrious working people; a prosperity that seemed to rest largely on the tenets of laissez-faire economics; and a Britannia that ruled the waves and many a dominion beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_Gladstone">After Gladstone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: After Gladstone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Gladstone finally retired in 1894. Gladstone\'s support for Home Rule deeply divided the party, and it lost its upper and upper-middle-class base, while keeping support among Protestant nonconformists and the Celtic fringe. Historian <a href="/wiki/R._C._K._Ensor" class="mw-redirect" title="R. C. K. Ensor">R. C. K. Ensor</a> reports that after 1886, the main Liberal Party was deserted by practically the entire whig peerage and the great majority of the upper-class and upper-middle-class members. High prestige London clubs that had a Liberal base were deeply split. Ensor notes that, "London society, following the known views of the Queen, practically ostracized home rulers." <sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new Liberal leader was the ineffectual <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery" title="Archibald Primrose, 5th Earl of Rosebery">Lord Rosebery</a>. He led the party to a heavy defeat in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1895" title="United Kingdom general election, 1895">1895 general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_factions">Liberal factions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Liberal factions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Liberal Party lacked a unified ideological base in 1906.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> It contained numerous contradictory and hostile factions, such as imperialists and supporters of the Boers;<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> near-socialists and laissez-faire classical liberals; suffragettes and opponents of women\'s suffrage;<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> antiwar elements and supporters of the <a href="/wiki/Entente_Cordiale" title="Entente Cordiale">military alliance with France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Non-Conformist Dissenters \xe2\x80\x93 Protestants outside the Anglican fold \xe2\x80\x93 were a powerful element, dedicated to opposing the established church in terms of education and taxation.  However, the Dissenters were losing support amid society at large and played a  lesser role in party affairs after 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The Party, furthermore, also included Irish Catholics, and secularists from the labour movement. Many Conservatives (including Winston Churchill) had recently protested against high tariff moves by the Conservatives by switching to the anti-tariff Liberal camp, but it was unclear how many old Conservative traits they brought along, especially on military and naval issues.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The middle-class business, professional and intellectual communities were generally strongholds, although some old aristocratic families played important roles as well. The working class element was moving rapidly toward the newly emerging Labour Party.  One uniting element was widespread agreement on the use of politics and Parliament as a device to upgrade and improve society and to reform politics. All Liberals were outraged when Conservatives used their majority in the House of Lords to block reform legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_rise_of_New_Liberalism">The rise of New Liberalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The rise of New Liberalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_welfare_reforms" title="Liberal welfare reforms">Liberal welfare reforms</a> and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People\'s Budget</a></div>\n<p>The late nineteenth century saw the emergence of \xe2\x80\x9cNew Liberalism\xe2\x80\x9d within the Liberal Party, which advocated state intervention as a means of guaranteeing freedom and removing obstacles to it such as poverty and unemployment. The policies of the New Liberalism are now known as <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">social liberalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg/220px-ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg/330px-ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg/440px-ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1808" data-file-height="2284" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Liberal politicians <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> enacted the 1909 <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People\'s Budget</a> which specifically aimed at the redistribution of wealth</div></div></div>\n<p>The New Liberals included intellectuals like <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Trelawny_Hobhouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Leonard Trelawny Hobhouse">L. T. Hobhouse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_A._Hobson" title="John A. Hobson">John A. Hobson</a>. They saw individual liberty as something achievable only under favourable social and economic circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-adams_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adams-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In their view, the poverty, squalor, and ignorance in which many people lived made it impossible for freedom and individuality to flourish. New Liberals believed that these conditions could be ameliorated only through collective action coordinated by a strong, welfare-oriented, and interventionist state.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the historic 1906 victory, the Liberal Party introduced multiple reforms on range of issues, including <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_insurance" class="mw-redirect" title="Unemployment insurance">unemployment insurance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pension" title="Pension">pensions</a> for elderly workers, thereby laying the groundwork for the future British <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>. Some proposals failed, such as licensing fewer pubs, or rolling back Conservative educational policies. The <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People\'s Budget</a> of 1909, championed by <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> and fellow Liberal <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, introduced unprecedented taxes on the wealthy in Britain and radical social welfare programmes to the country\'s policies.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It was the first budget with the expressed intent of redistributing wealth among the public. It imposed increased taxes on luxuries, liquor, tobacco, high incomes, and land -- taxation that disproportionately affected the rich. The new money was to be made available for new welfare programmes as well as new battleships. In 1911 Lloyd George succeeded in putting through Parliament his <a href="/wiki/National_Insurance_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="National Insurance Act">National Insurance Act</a>, making provision for sickness and invalidism, and this was followed by his <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_Insurance_Act_1920" title="Unemployment Insurance Act 1920">Unemployment Insurance Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian Peter Weiler argues that:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Although still partially informed by older Liberal concerns for character, self-reliance, and the capitalist market, this legislation nevertheless, marked a significant shift in Liberal approaches to the state and social reform, approaches that later governments would slowly expand and that would grow into the welfare state after the Second World War. What was new in these reforms was the underlying assumption that the state could be a positive force, that the measure of individual freedom... was not how much the state left people alone, but whether it gave them the capacity to fill themselves as individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Contrasting Old Liberalism with New Liberalism, David Lloyd George noted in a 1908 speech that the old Liberals:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>used the natural discontent of the people with the poverty and precariousness of the means of subsistence as a motive power to win for them a better, more influential, and more honourable status in the citizenship of their native land. The new Liberalism, while pursuing this great political ideal with unflinching energy, devotes a part of its endeavour also to the removing of the immediate causes of discontent. It is true that man cannot live by bread alone. It is equally true that a man cannot live without bread.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Liberal_zenith">The Liberal zenith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The Liberal zenith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_welfare_reforms" title="Liberal welfare reforms">Liberal welfare reforms</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg/220px-United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg.png" width="220" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg/330px-United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg/440px-United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1166" data-file-height="1211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_general_election_1906.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The results of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1906" title="United Kingdom general election, 1906">1906 election</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg/220px-Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg" width="220" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg/330px-Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg/440px-Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1167" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ten_Years_of_Toryism.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Liberal poster c. 1905\xe2\x80\x931910, clockwise from the left: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> abandons his commitment to old age pensions; Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Austen_Chamberlain" title="Austen Chamberlain">Austen Chamberlain</a> threatens duties on consumer items which had been removed by Gladstone (in the picture on the wall); Chinese indentured labour in South Africa; <a href="/wiki/John_Bull" title="John Bull">John Bull</a> contemplates his vote; and Joseph Chamberlain and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a> (who favoured retaliatory tariffs) wearing top hats</div></div></div>\n<p>The Liberals languished in opposition for a decade, while the coalition of Salisbury and Chamberlain held power. The 1890s were marred by infighting between the three principal successors to Gladstone, party leader <a href="/wiki/William_Vernon_Harcourt_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Vernon Harcourt (politician)">William Harcourt</a>, former Prime Minister Lord Rosebery, and Gladstone\'s personal secretary, <a href="/wiki/John_Morley" title="John Morley">John Morley</a>. This intrigue finally led Harcourt and Morley to resign their positions in 1898 as they continued to be at loggerheads with Rosebery over Irish home rule and issues relating to imperialism. Replacing Harcourt as party leader was Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a>. Harcourt\'s resignation briefly muted the turmoil in the party, but the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> soon nearly broke the party apart, with Rosebery and a circle of supporters including important future Liberal figures H.H. Asquith, Edward Grey, and Richard Burdon Haldane forming a clique dubbed the "Liberal Imperialists" that supported the government in the prosecution of the war. On the other side, more radical members of the party formed a Pro-Boer faction that denounced the conflict and called for an immediate end to hostilities. Quickly rising to prominence among the Pro-Boers was David Lloyd George, a relatively new MP and a master of rhetoric, who took advantage of having a national stage to speak out on a controversial issue to make his name in the party. Harcourt and Morley also sided with this group, though with slightly different aims. Campbell-Bannerman tried to keep these forces together at the head of a moderate Liberal rump, but in 1901 he delivered a speech on the government\'s "methods of barbarism" in South Africa that pulled him further to the left and nearly tore the party in two. The party was saved after Salisbury\'s retirement in 1902 when his successor, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, pushed a series of unpopular initiatives such as the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1902" title="Education Act 1902">Education Act 1902</a> and Joseph Chamberlain called for a new system of protectionist tariffs.\n</p><p>Campbell-Bannerman was able to rally the party around the traditional liberal platform of free trade and land reform and led them to <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1906" title="United Kingdom general election, 1906">the greatest election victory</a> in their history. This would prove the last time the Liberals won a majority in their own right.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  Although he presided over a large majority, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a> was overshadowed by his ministers, most notably <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> at the Exchequer, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon" title="Edward Grey, 1st Viscount Grey of Fallodon">Edward Grey</a> at the Foreign Office, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burdon_Haldane,_1st_Viscount_Haldane" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Burdon Haldane, 1st Viscount Haldane">Richard Burdon Haldane</a> at the War Office and <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> at the Board of Trade. Campbell-Bannerman retired in 1908 and died soon after. He was succeeded by Asquith, who stepped up the government\'s radicalism. Lloyd George succeeded Asquith at the Exchequer, and was in turn succeeded at the Board of Trade by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, a recent defector from the Conservatives.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith_Q_42036.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Asquith_Q_42036.jpg/220px-Asquith_Q_42036.jpg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Asquith_Q_42036.jpg/330px-Asquith_Q_42036.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Asquith_Q_42036.jpg/440px-Asquith_Q_42036.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asquith_Q_42036.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The General Election of 1906 also represented a shift to the left by the Liberal Party. According to Rosemary Rees, almost half of the Liberal MPs elected in 1906 were supportive of the \'New Liberalism\' (which advocated government action to improve people\'s lives),<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>) while claims were made that \xe2\x80\x9cfive-sixths of the Liberal party are left wing.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Other historians, however, have questioned the extent to which the Liberal Party experienced a leftward shift; according to Robert C. Self however, only between 50 and 60 Liberal MPs out of the 400 in the parliamentary party after 1906 were Social Radicals, with a core of 20 to 30.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, important junior offices were held in the cabinet by what Duncan Tanner has termed "genuine New Liberals, Centrist reformers, and Fabian collectivists,"<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and much legislation was pushed through by the Liberals in government. This included the regulation of working hours, <a href="/wiki/National_Insurance" title="National Insurance">National Insurance</a> and welfare.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Punch_cartoon_28_April_1909.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Punch_cartoon_28_April_1909.jpg/280px-Punch_cartoon_28_April_1909.jpg" width="280" height="345" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/cf/Punch_cartoon_28_April_1909.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="406" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Punch_cartoon_28_April_1909.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cartoonist <a href="/wiki/John_Bernard_Partridge" class="mw-redirect" title="John Bernard Partridge">John Bernard Partridge</a> depicts Lloyd George as a giant with a cudgel labelled "Budget" in reference to his <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People\'s Budget</a> while Asquith cowers beneath the table, <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i> 28 April 1909</div></div></div>\n<p>A political battle erupted over the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People\'s Budget</a> and resulted in the passage of an act ending the power of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> to block legislation. The cost was high, however, as the government was required by the king to call two general elections in 1910 to validate its position and ended up frittering away most of its large majority, being left once again dependent on the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Nationalists</a>.\n</p><p>As a result, Asquith was forced to introduce a new <a href="/wiki/Third_Home_Rule_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Home Rule Act">third Home Rule bill</a> in 1912. Since the House of Lords no longer had the power to block the bill, the Unionist\'s <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteers" title="Ulster Volunteers">Ulster Volunteers</a> led by Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carson" title="Edward Carson">Edward Carson</a>, launched a campaign of opposition that included the threat of armed resistance in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Ulster</a> and the threat of mass resignation of their commissions by army officers in Ireland in 1914 (<i>see <a href="/wiki/Curragh_Incident" class="mw-redirect" title="Curragh Incident">Curragh Incident</a></i>). In their resistance to Home Rule the Ulster Protestants had the full support of the Conservatives, whose leader, <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>, was of <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_people" title="Ulster Scots people">Ulster-Scots</a> descent. The country seemed to be on the brink of civil war when the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> broke out in August 1914. Historian <a href="/wiki/George_Dangerfield" title="George Dangerfield">George Dangerfield</a> has argued that the multiplicity of crises in 1910 to 1914, before the war broke out, so weakened the Liberal coalition that it marked the <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Death_of_Liberal_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Strange Death of Liberal England">Strange Death of Liberal England</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> However, most historians date the collapse to the crisis of the First World War.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline">Decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War" title="History of the United Kingdom during the First World War">History of the United Kingdom during the First World War</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a></div>\n<p>The Liberal Party might have survived a short war, but the totality of the Great War called for measures that the Party had long  rejected.  The result was the permanent destruction of ability of the Liberal Party to lead a government.  Historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Blake,_Baron_Blake" title="Robert Blake, Baron Blake">Robert Blake</a> explains the dilemma:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>the Liberals were traditionally the party of freedom of speech, conscience and trade.  They were against jingoism, heavy armaments and compulsion....Liberals were neither wholehearted nor unanimous about conscription, censorship,  the <a href="/wiki/Defence_of_the_Realm_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Defence of the Realm Act">Defence of the Realm Act</a>, severity toward aliens and pacifists, direction of labour and industry.  The Conservatives... had no such misgivings.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Blake further notes that it was the Liberals, not the Conservatives who needed the moral outrage of Belgium to justify going to war, while the Conservatives called for intervention from the start of the crisis on the grounds of <i>realpolitik</i> and the balance of power.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>   Lloyd George and Churchill, however, were zealous supporters of the war, and gradually forced the old peace-orientated Liberals out. \n</p><p>Asquith was blamed for the poor British performance in first year. Since the Liberals ran the war without consulting the Unionists (Conservatives) there were heavy partisan attacks.  However even Liberal commentators were dismayed by the lack of energy at the top. At the time public opinion was intensely hostile, both in the media and in the street, against any young man in civilian garb and labeled as a slacker. The leading Liberal newspaper, the <i>Manchester Guardian</i> complained&#160;:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The fact that the Government has not dared to challenge the nation to rise above itself, is one among many signs... The war is, in fact, not being taken seriously.... How can any slacker be blamed when the Government itself is slack.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Asquith\'s Liberal government was brought down in <span class="nowrap">May 1915</span>, due in particular to a <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">crisis in inadequate artillery shell production</a> and the protest resignation of <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">Admiral Fisher</a> over the disastrous  <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli Campaign</a> against Turkey. Reluctant to face doom in an election, Asquith formed a new coalition government on <span class="nowrap">25 May</span>, with the majority of the new cabinet coming from his own Liberal party and the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Unionist (Conservative) party</a>, along with a token Labour representation. The new government lasted a year and a half, and was the last time Liberals controlled the government.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The analysis of historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> is that the British people were so deeply divided over numerous issues, But on all sides there was growing distrust for the Asquith government. There was no agreement whatsoever on wartime issues. The leaders of the two parties realized that embittered debates in Parliament would further undermine  popular morale, and so the House of Commons did not once discuss the war before May 1915. Taylor argues:<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<dl><dd>The Unionists, by and large, regarded Germany as a dangerous rival, and rejoiced at the chance to destroy her. They meant to fight a hard-headed war by ruthless methods; they condemned Liberal \'softness\' before the war and now. The Liberals insisted on remaining high-minded. Many of them had come to support the war only when the Germans invaded Belgium....Entering the war for idealistic motives, the Liberals wished to fight it by noble means and found it harder to abandon their principles than to endure defeat in the field.</dd></dl>\n<p>The 1915 coalition fell apart at the end of 1916, when the Conservatives withdrew their support from Asquith and gave it instead to Lloyd George, who became Prime Minister at the head of a new coalition largely made up of Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Asquith and his followers moved to the opposition benches in Parliament and the Liberal Party was deeply split once again.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lloyd_George_as_a_Liberal_heading_a_Conservative_coalition">Lloyd George as a Liberal heading a Conservative coalition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lloyd George as a Liberal heading a Conservative coalition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Lloyd George remained a Liberal all his life, but he abandoned many standard Liberal principles in his crusade to win the war at all costs. He insisted on strong government controls over business, as opposed to the laissez-faire attitudes of traditional Liberals. He insisted on conscription of young men into the Army, a position that deeply troubled his old colleagues. That brought him and a few like-minded Liberals into the new coalition on the ground long occupied by Conservatives. There was no more planning for world peace or liberal treatment of Germany, nor discomfit with aggressive and authoritarian measures of state power. More deadly to the future of the Party, says historian Trevor Wilson, was its repudiation by ideological Liberals, who decided sadly that it no longer represented their principles. Finally the presence of the vigorous new Labour Party on the left gave a new home to voters disenchanted with the Liberal performance.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/David_Lloyd_George.jpg/220px-David_Lloyd_George.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/David_Lloyd_George.jpg/330px-David_Lloyd_George.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/David_Lloyd_George.jpg/440px-David_Lloyd_George.jpg 2x" data-file-width="907" data-file-height="1182" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Lloyd George</div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1918" title="United Kingdom general election, 1918">1918 general election</a> Lloyd George, hailed as "the Man Who Won the War", led his coalition into a <i><a href="/wiki/Khaki_election" title="Khaki election">khaki election</a></i>.  Lloyd George and the Conservative leader <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> wrote a joint letter of support to candidates to indicate they were considered the official Coalition candidates \xe2\x80\x93 this <a href="/wiki/The_Coalition_Coupon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Coalition Coupon">"coupon"</a>, as it became known, was issued against many sitting Liberal MPs, often to devastating effect, though not against Asquith himself. The coalition won a massive victory as the Asquithian Liberals and  Labour were decimated. Those remaining Liberal MPs who were opposed to the Coalition Government went into opposition under the parliamentary leadership of <a href="/wiki/Donald_Maclean_(British_politician)" title="Donald Maclean (British politician)">Sir Donald MacLean</a> who also became <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>. Asquith, who had appointed MacLean, remained as overall Leader of the Liberal Party even though he lost his seat in 1918. Asquith returned to parliament in 1920 and resumed leadership. Between 1919-1923, the anti-Lloyd George  Liberals were called Asquithian Liberals, <a href="/wiki/Wee_Free" title="Wee Free">Wee Free</a> Liberals<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Independent_Liberal_Party_(UK,_1918)" title="Independent Liberal Party (UK, 1918)">Independent Liberals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lloyd George was increasingly under the influence of the rejuvenated Conservative party who numerically dominated the coalition. In 1922, the Conservative backbenchers <a href="/wiki/Carlton_Club_meeting" title="Carlton Club meeting">rebelled</a> against the continuation of the coalition, citing in particular Lloyd George\'s plan for war with Turkey in the <a href="/wiki/Chanak_Crisis" title="Chanak Crisis">Chanak Crisis</a>, and his corrupt sale of honours. He resigned as Prime Minister and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>.\n</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1922" title="United Kingdom general election, 1922">1922</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1923" title="United Kingdom general election, 1923">1923 elections</a> the Liberals won barely a third of the vote and only a quarter of the seats in the House of Commons, as many radical voters abandoned the divided Liberals and went over to Labour. In 1922 Labour became the official opposition. A reunion of the two warring factions took place in 1923 when the new Conservative Prime Minister Stanley Baldwin committed his party to protective tariffs, causing the Liberals to reunite in support of free trade. The party gained ground in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1923" title="United Kingdom general election, 1923">1923 general election</a> but made most of its gains from Conservatives whilst losing ground to Labour \xe2\x80\x93 a sign of the party\'s direction for many years to come. The party remained the third largest in the House of Commons, but the Conservatives had lost their majority. There was much speculation and fear about the prospect of a Labour government, and comparatively little about a Liberal government, even though it could have plausibly presented an experienced team of ministers compared to Labour\'s almost complete lack of experience, as well as offering a middle ground that could obtain support from both Conservatives and Labour in crucial Commons divisions. But instead of trying to force the opportunity to form a Liberal government, Asquith decided instead to allow Labour the chance of office, in the belief that they would prove incompetent and this would set the stage for a revival of Liberal fortunes at Labour\'s expense. It was a fatal error.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_popular_vote.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/UK_popular_vote.svg/300px-UK_popular_vote.svg.png" width="300" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/UK_popular_vote.svg/450px-UK_popular_vote.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/UK_popular_vote.svg/600px-UK_popular_vote.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="543" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_popular_vote.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of the vote received by Conservatives (blue), Whigs/Liberals/Liberal Democrats (orange), Labour (red) and others (grey) in general elections since 1832<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> shows that following success as the successor to the Whig party, the party\'s share of the popular vote plummeted after the First World War as it lost votes to the new Labour party and fractured into groups such as the National and Coalition Liberals</div></div></div>\n<p>Labour was determined to destroy the Liberals and become the sole party of the left. <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> was forced into a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1924" title="United Kingdom general election, 1924">snap election in 1924</a>, and although his government was defeated, he achieved his objective of virtually wiping the Liberals out as many more radical voters now moved to Labour, whilst moderate middle-class Liberal voters concerned about socialism moved to the Conservatives. The Liberals were reduced to a mere forty seats in Parliament, only seven of which had been won against candidates from both parties; and none of these formed a coherent area of Liberal survival. The party seemed finished, and during this period some Liberals, such as Churchill, went over to the Conservatives, while others went over to Labour. (Several Labour ministers of later generations, such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a>, were the sons of Liberal MPs.)\n</p><p>Asquith died in 1928 and the enigmatic figure of Lloyd George returned to the leadership and began a drive to produce coherent policies on many key issues of the day. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1929" title="United Kingdom general election, 1929">1929 general election</a> he made a final bid to return the Liberals to the political mainstream, with an ambitious programme of state stimulation of the economy called <i>We Can Conquer Unemployment!</i>, largely written for him by the Liberal economist <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>. The Liberals gained ground, but once again it was at the Conservatives\' expense whilst also losing seats to Labour. Indeed, the urban areas of the country suffering heavily from unemployment, which might have been expected to respond the most to the radical economic policies of the Liberals, instead gave the party its worst results. By contrast most of the party\'s seats were won either due to the absence of a candidate from one of the other parties or in rural areas on the "<a href="/wiki/Celtic_fringe" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic fringe">Celtic fringe</a>", where local evidence suggests that economic ideas were at best peripheral to the electorate\'s concerns. The Liberals now found themselves with 59 members, holding the balance of power in a Parliament where Labour was the largest party but lacked an overall majority. Lloyd George offered a degree of support to the Labour government in the hope of winning concessions, including a degree of electoral reform to introduce the <a href="/wiki/Instant-runoff_voting" title="Instant-runoff voting">alternative vote</a>, but this support was to prove bitterly divisive as the Liberals increasingly divided between those seeking to gain what Liberal goals they could achieve, those who preferred a Conservative government to a Labour one and vice versa.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The last majority Liberal Government in Britain was elected in 1906.\nThe years preceding the First World War were marked by worker strikes and civil unrest and saw many violent confrontations between civilians and the police and armed forces. Other issues of the period included <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">women\'s suffrage</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a> movement.\nAfter the carnage of 1914\xe2\x80\x931918, the democratic reforms of the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a> instantly tripled the number of people entitled to vote in Britain from seven to twenty-one million. The Labour Party benefited most from this huge change in the electorate, forming its <a href="/wiki/First_Labour_Government_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="First Labour Government (UK)">first minority government in 1924</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_splits_over_the_National_Government">The splits over the National Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The splits over the National Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gws_samuel_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Gws_samuel_01.jpg/140px-Gws_samuel_01.jpg" width="140" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Gws_samuel_01.jpg/210px-Gws_samuel_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Gws_samuel_01.jpg/280px-Gws_samuel_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gws_samuel_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Samuel,_1st_Viscount_Samuel" title="Herbert Samuel, 1st Viscount Samuel">Herbert Samuel</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1931 MacDonald\'s government fell apart in response to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Great Depression in the United Kingdom">Great Depression</a>, and the Liberals agreed to join his <a href="/wiki/National_Government_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Government (United Kingdom)">National Government</a>, dominated by the Conservatives. Lloyd George himself was ill and did not actually join. Soon, however, the Liberals faced another divisive crisis when a National Government was proposed to fight the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1931" title="United Kingdom general election, 1931">1931 general election</a> with a mandate for tariffs. From the outside, Lloyd George called for the party to abandon the government completely in defence of free trade, but <a href="/wiki/Independent_Liberals_(UK,_1931)" title="Independent Liberals (UK, 1931)">only a few MPs and candidates followed</a>. Another group under <a href="/wiki/John_Simon,_1st_Viscount_Simon" title="John Simon, 1st Viscount Simon">Sir John Simon</a> then emerged, who were prepared to continue their support for the government and take the Liberal places in the Cabinet if there were resignations. The third group under <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Samuel,_1st_Viscount_Samuel" title="Herbert Samuel, 1st Viscount Samuel">Sir Herbert Samuel</a> pressed for the parties in government to fight the election on separate platforms. In doing so the bulk of Liberals remained supporting the government, but two distinct Liberal groups had emerged within this bulk \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberal_Party_(UK,_1931)" title="National Liberal Party (UK, 1931)">Liberal Nationals</a> (officially the "National Liberals" after 1947) led by Simon, also known as "Simonites", and the "Samuelites" or "official Liberals", led by Samuel who remained as the official party. Both groups secured about 34 MPs but proceeded to diverge even further after the election, with the Liberal Nationals remaining supporters of the government throughout its life. There were to be a succession of discussions about them rejoining the Liberals, but these usually foundered on the issues of free trade and continued support for the National Government. The one significant reunification came in 1946 when the Liberal and Liberal National party organisations in London merged.\n</p><p>The official Liberals found themselves a tiny minority within a government committed to <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>. Slowly they found this issue to be one they could not support. In early 1932 it was agreed to suspend the principle of <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_collective_responsibility" title="Cabinet collective responsibility">collective responsibility</a> to allow the Liberals to oppose the introduction of tariffs. Later in 1932 the Liberals resigned their ministerial posts over the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottawa Conference">Ottawa Agreement</a> on <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Preference" title="Imperial Preference">Imperial Preference</a>. However, they remained sitting on the government benches supporting it in Parliament, though in the country local Liberal activists bitterly opposed the government. Finally in late 1933 the Liberals crossed the floor of the House of Commons and went into complete opposition. By this point their number of MPs was severely depleted. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1935" title="United Kingdom general election, 1935">1935 general election</a>, just 17 Liberal MPs were elected, along with Lloyd George and three followers as "<a href="/wiki/Independent_Liberals_(UK,_1931)" title="Independent Liberals (UK, 1931)">independent Liberals</a>". Immediately after the election the two groups reunited, though Lloyd George declined to play much of a formal role in his old party. Over the next ten years there would be further defections as MPs deserted to either the Liberal Nationals or Labour. Yet there were a few recruits, such as <a href="/wiki/Clement_Davies" title="Clement Davies">Clement Davies</a>, who had deserted to the National Liberals in 1931 but now returned to the party during the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> and who would lead it after the war.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Near_extinction">Near extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Near extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Samuel had lost his seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1935" title="United Kingdom general election, 1935">1935 election</a> and the leadership of the party fell to <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Sinclair,_1st_Viscount_Thurso" title="Archibald Sinclair, 1st Viscount Thurso">Sir Archibald Sinclair</a>. With many traditional domestic Liberal policies now regarded as irrelevant, he focused the party on opposition to both the rise of Fascism in Europe and the <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a> foreign policy of the British government, arguing that intervention was needed, in contrast to the Labour calls for pacifism. Despite the party\'s weaknesses, Sinclair gained a high profile as he sought to recall the <a href="/wiki/Midlothian_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Midlothian Campaign">Midlothian Campaign</a> and once more revitalise the Liberals as the party of a strong foreign policy.\n</p><p>In 1940 they joined Churchill\'s wartime coalition government, with Sinclair serving as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Air" title="Secretary of State for Air">Secretary of State for Air</a>, the last British Liberal to hold Cabinet rank office for seventy years. However, it was a sign of the party\'s lack of importance that they were not included in the <a href="/wiki/War_Cabinet" class="mw-redirect" title="War Cabinet">War Cabinet</a>; some leading party members founded <a href="/wiki/Radical_Action" title="Radical Action">Radical Action</a>, a group which called for liberal candidates to break the <a href="/wiki/War-time_electoral_pact" title="War-time electoral pact">war-time electoral pact</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">1945 general election</a>, Sinclair and many of his colleagues lost their seats to both Conservatives and Labour, and the party returned just 12 MPs to Westminster.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> But this was just the beginning of the decline.
