In <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1950" title="United Kingdom general election, 1950">1950</a>, the general election saw the Liberals return just nine MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Another <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">general election was called in 1951</a>, and the Liberals were left with just six MPs; all but one of them were aided by the fact that the Conservatives refrained from fielding candidates in those constituencies.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1957 this total fell to five when one of the Liberal MPs died and the subsequent by-election was lost to the Labour Party, which selected the former Liberal Deputy Leader <a href="/wiki/Megan_Lloyd_George" title="Megan Lloyd George">Megan Lloyd George</a> as its own candidate. The Liberal Party seemed close to extinction. During this low period, it was often joked that Liberal MPs could hold meetings in the back of one taxi.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_revival">Liberal revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Liberal revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jo_Grimond.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Jo_Grimond.jpg/140px-Jo_Grimond.jpg" width="140" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/Jo_Grimond.jpg/210px-Jo_Grimond.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/67/Jo_Grimond.jpg 2x" data-file-width="220" data-file-height="325" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jo_Grimond.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jo_Grimond" title="Jo Grimond">Jo Grimond</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Through the 1950s and into the 1960s the Liberals survived only because a handful of constituencies in rural <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> clung to their Liberal traditions, whilst in two English towns, <a href="/wiki/Bolton" title="Bolton">Bolton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield" title="Huddersfield">Huddersfield</a>, local Liberals and Conservatives agreed to each contest only one of the town\'s two seats. <a href="/wiki/Jo_Grimond" title="Jo Grimond">Jo Grimond</a>, for example, who became Liberal leader in 1956, was MP for the remote <a href="/wiki/Orkney_and_Shetland_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Orkney and Shetland (UK Parliament constituency)">Orkney and Shetland</a> islands. Under his leadership a Liberal revival began, marked by the <a href="/wiki/Orpington_by-election,_1962" title="Orpington by-election, 1962">Orpington by-election</a> of March 1962 which was won by <a href="/wiki/Eric_Reginald_Lubbock,_4th_Baron_Avebury" class="mw-redirect" title="Eric Reginald Lubbock, 4th Baron Avebury">Eric Lubbock</a>. There, the Liberals won a seat in the London suburbs for the first time since 1935.\n</p><p>The Liberals became the first of the major British political parties to advocate British membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>. Grimond also sought an intellectual revival of the party, seeking to position it as a non-socialist radical alternative to the Conservative government of the day. In particular he canvassed the support of the young post-war university students and recent graduates, appealing to younger voters in a way that many of his recent predecessors had not, and asserting a new strand of Liberalism for the post-war world.\n</p><p>The new middle-class suburban generation began to find the Liberals\' policies attractive again. Under Grimond (who retired in 1967) and his successor, <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Thorpe" title="Jeremy Thorpe">Jeremy Thorpe</a>, the Liberals regained the status of a serious third force in British politics, polling up to 20% of the vote but unable to break the duopoly of Labour and Conservative and win more than fourteen seats in the Commons. An additional problem was competition in the Liberal heartlands in Scotland and Wales from the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a> who both grew as electoral forces from the 1960s onwards. Although <a href="/wiki/Emlyn_Hooson" class="mw-redirect" title="Emlyn Hooson">Emlyn Hooson</a> held on to the seat of Montgomeryshire, upon <a href="/wiki/Clement_Davies" title="Clement Davies">Clement Davies</a> death in 1962, the party lost five Welsh seats between 1950 and 1966. In September 1966 the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Liberal_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Liberal Party">Welsh Liberal Party</a> formed their own state party, moving the Liberal Party into a fully federal structure.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In local elections <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> remained a Liberal stronghold, with the party taking the plurality of seats on the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_1973" title="United Kingdom local elections, 1973">elections</a> to the new Liverpool Metropolitan Borough Council in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">February 1974 general election</a> the Conservative government of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> won a plurality of votes cast, but the Labour Party gained a plurality of seats due to the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist</a> MPs refusing to support the Conservatives after the Northern Ireland <a href="/wiki/Sunningdale_Agreement" title="Sunningdale Agreement">Sunningdale Agreement</a>. The Liberals now held the balance of power in the Commons. Conservatives offered Thorpe the <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> if he would join a coalition government with Heath. Thorpe was personally in favour of it, but the party insisted on a clear government commitment to introducing <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> and a change of Prime Minister. The former was unacceptable to Heath\'s Cabinet and the latter to Heath personally, so the talks collapsed. Instead a minority Labour government was formed under <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> but with no formal support from Thorpe. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_October_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, October 1974">October 1974 general election</a> the Liberals slipped back slightly and the Labour government won a wafer-thin majority.\n</p><p>Thorpe was subsequently forced to resign after allegations that he attempted to have his homosexual lover murdered by a hitman. The party\'s new leader, <a href="/wiki/David_Steel" title="David Steel">David Steel</a>, negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Lib-Lab_pact" class="mw-redirect" title="Lib-Lab pact">Lib-Lab pact</a> with Wilson\'s successor as Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>. According to this pact, the Liberals would support the government in crucial votes in exchange for some influence over policy. The agreement lasted from 1977 to 1978, but proved mostly fruitless, for two reasons: the Liberals\' key demand of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> was rejected by most Labour MPs, whilst the contacts between Liberal spokespersons and Labour ministers often proved detrimental, such as between finance spokesperson <a href="/wiki/John_Pardoe" title="John Pardoe">John Pardoe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Denis_Healey" title="Denis Healey">Denis Healey</a>, who were mutually antagonistic.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alliance_and_Liberal_Democrats">Alliance and Liberal Democrats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Alliance and Liberal Democrats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/SDP-Liberal_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="SDP-Liberal Alliance">SDP-Liberal Alliance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats (UK)</a></div>\n<p>The Conservative Party under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">1979 general election</a>, placing the Labour Party back in opposition, which served to push the Liberals back into the margins.\n</p><p>In 1981, defectors from a moderate faction of the Labour Party, led by former Cabinet ministers <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Owen" title="David Owen">David Owen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Williams" title="Shirley Williams">Shirley Williams</a>, founded the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a> (SDP). The new party and the Liberals quickly formed the SDP-Liberal Alliance, which for a while polled as high as 50% in the opinion polls and appeared capable of winning the next general election. Indeed, Steel was so confident of an Alliance victory that he told the 1981 Liberal conference, "Go back to your constituencies, and prepare for government!"<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the Alliance was overtaken in the polls by the Tories in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Falkland Islands War">Falkland Islands War</a> and at the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">1983 general election</a> the Conservatives were re-elected by a landslide, with Labour once again forming the opposition. While the SDP-Liberal Alliance came close to Labour in terms of votes (a share of more than 25%), it only had 23 MPs compared to Labour\'s 209.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987 general election</a>, the Alliance\'s share of the votes fell slightly and it now had 22 MPs. In the election\'s aftermath Steel proposed a merger of the two parties. Most SDP members voted in favour of the merger, but SDP leader <a href="/wiki/David_Owen" title="David Owen">David Owen</a> objected and continued to lead a "rump" SDP.\n</p><p>In March 1988 the Liberal Party and Social Democratic Party merged to create the Social and Liberal Democrats, renamed the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> in October 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Over two-thirds of the members, and all the serving MPs, of the Liberal Party joined this party, led first jointly by Steel and the SDP leader <a href="/wiki/Robert_Maclennan,_Baron_Maclennan_of_Rogart" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Maclennan, Baron Maclennan of Rogart">Robert Maclennan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A group of Liberal opponents of the merger with the Social Democrats, including <a href="/wiki/Michael_Meadowcroft" title="Michael Meadowcroft">Michael Meadowcroft</a> (the former Liberal MP for Leeds West) and Paul Wiggin (who served on <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_Peterborough" class="mw-redirect" title="Local government in Peterborough">Peterborough City Council</a> as a Liberal), continued with a new party organisation under the old name of "the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK,_1989)" title="Liberal Party (UK, 1989)">Liberal Party</a>". Meadowcroft joined the Liberal Democrats in 2007.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology">Ideology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ideology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During the 19th century, the Liberal Party was broadly in favour of what would today be called <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberalism</a>: supporting <i><a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a></i> economic policies such as <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> and minimal government interference in the economy (this doctrine was usually termed \'<a href="/wiki/Gladstonian_Liberalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Gladstonian Liberalism">Gladstonian Liberalism</a>\' after the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> Liberal Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>). The Liberal Party favoured social reform, personal liberty, reducing the powers of <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> (many of them were <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">Nonconformists</a>) and an extension of the electoral <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">franchise</a>. Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Vernon_Harcourt_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Vernon Harcourt (politician)">William Harcourt</a>, a prominent Liberal politician in the Victorian era, said this about <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> in 1872:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leeds_Town_Hall,_General_Election_results.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Leeds_Town_Hall%2C_General_Election_results.jpg/220px-Leeds_Town_Hall%2C_General_Election_results.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Leeds_Town_Hall%2C_General_Election_results.jpg/330px-Leeds_Town_Hall%2C_General_Election_results.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Leeds_Town_Hall%2C_General_Election_results.jpg/440px-Leeds_Town_Hall%2C_General_Election_results.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="330" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leeds_Town_Hall,_General_Election_results.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A crowd waits outside <a href="/wiki/Leeds_Town_Hall" title="Leeds Town Hall">Leeds Town Hall</a> to see them elect a Liberal Party candidate during the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1880" title="United Kingdom general election, 1880">1880 general elections</a></div></div></div>\n<blockquote><p>If there be any party which is more pledged than another to resist a policy of restrictive legislation, having for its object social coercion, that party is the Liberal party. (Cheers.) But liberty does not consist in making others do what you think right, (Hear, hear.) The difference between a free Government and a Government which is not free is principally this\xe2\x80\x94that a Government which is not free interferes with everything it can, and a free Government interferes with nothing except what it must. A despotic Government tries to make everybody do what it wishes; a Liberal Government tries, as far as the safety of society will permit, to allow everybody to do as he wishes. It has been the tradition of the Liberal party consistently to maintain the doctrine of individual liberty. It is because they have done so that England is the place where people can do more what they please than in any other country in the world...It is this practice of allowing one set of people to dictate to another set of people what they shall do, what they shall think, what they shall drink, when they shall go to bed, what they shall buy, and where they shall buy it, what wages they shall get and how they shall spend them, against which the Liberal party have always protested.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The political terms of "modern", "progressive" or "new" Liberalism began to appear in the mid to late 1880s and became increasingly common to denote the tendency in the Liberal Party to favour an increased role for the state as more important than the classical liberal stress on self-help and freedom of choice.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the early 20th century the Liberals stance began to shift towards "New Liberalism", what would today be called <a href="/wiki/Social_liberalism" title="Social liberalism">social liberalism</a>: a belief in personal liberty with a support for government intervention to provide minimum levels of <a href="/wiki/Welfare" title="Welfare">welfare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> This shift was best exemplified by the Liberal government of <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> and his Chancellor <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Liberal_reforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal reforms">Liberal reforms</a> in the early 1900s created a basic <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>David Lloyd George adopted a programme at the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1929" title="United Kingdom general election, 1929">1929 general election</a> entitled <i>We Can Conquer Unemployment!</i>, although by this stage the Liberals had declined to third-party status. The Liberals now (as expressed in the Liberal Yellow Book) regarded opposition to state intervention as being a characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right-wing</a> extremists.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After nearly becoming extinct in the 1940s and 50s, the Liberal Party revived its fortunes somewhat under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Jo_Grimond" title="Jo Grimond">Jo Grimond</a> in the 1960s, by positioning itself as a <a href="/wiki/Radical_center_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radical center (politics)">radical centrist</a> non-<a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> alternative to the Conservative and Labour Party governments of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_alignment">Religious alignment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Party_(UK)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Religious alignment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since 1660, <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">Nonconformist</a> Protestants have played a major role in English politics. Relatively few MPs were dissenters. However the Dissenters were a major voting bloc in many areas, such as the East Midlands.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> They were very well organised and highly motivated and largely won over the Whigs and Liberals to their cause. Down to the 1830s, Dissenters demanded removal of <a href="/wiki/Political_and_civil_disabilities" class="mw-redirect" title="Political and civil disabilities">political and civil disabilities</a> that applied to them (especially those in the Test and Corporation Acts). The Anglican establishment strongly resisted until 1828. Numerous reforms of voting rights, especially that of 1832, increased the political power of Dissenters. They demanded an end to compulsory church rates, in which local taxes went only to Anglican churches. They finally achieved the end of religious tests for university degrees in 1905. Gladstone brought the majority of dissenters around to support for Home Rule for Ireland, putting the dissenting Protestants in league with the Irish Roman Catholics in an otherwise unlikely alliance. The dissenters gave significant support to moralistic issues, such as temperance and sabbath enforcement. The <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_conscience" title="Nonconformist conscience">nonconformist conscience</a>, as it was called, was repeatedly called upon by Gladstone for support for his moralistic foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In election after election, Protestant ministers rallied their congregations to the Liberal ticket. In Scotland, the Presbyterians played a similar role to the Nonconformist Methodists, Baptists and other groups in England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the 1820s the different Nonconformists, including <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyan Methodists</a>, Baptists, Congregationalists, and Unitarians, had formed the Committee of Dissenting Deputies and agitated for repeal of the highly restrictive <a href="/wiki/Test_and_Corporation_Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="Test and Corporation Acts">Test and Corporation Acts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> These Acts excluded Nonconformists from holding civil or military office or attending Oxford or Cambridge, compelling them to set up their own <a href="/wiki/Dissenting_Academies" class="mw-redirect" title="Dissenting Academies">Dissenting Academies</a> privately.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tories_(British_political_party)" title="Tories (British political party)">Tories</a> tended to be in favour of these Acts and so the Nonconformist cause was linked closely to the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a>, who advocated civil and religious liberty. After the Test and Corporation Acts were <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_Test_Act_1828" title="Sacramental Test Act 1828">repealed in 1828</a>, all the Nonconformists elected to Parliament were Liberals.\n</p><p>Nonconformists were angered by the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1902" title="Education Act 1902">Education Act 1902</a>, which integrated Church of England denominational schools into the state system and provided for their support from taxes. <a href="/wiki/John_Clifford_(minister)" title="John Clifford (minister)">John Clifford</a> formed the National Passive Resistance Committee and by 1906 over 170 Nonconformists had gone to prison for refusing to pay school taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> They included 60 <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Methodists" class="mw-redirect" title="Primitive Methodists">Primitive Methodists</a>, 48 Baptists, 40 Congregationalists, and 15 Wesleyan Methodists.\n</p><p>The political strength of Dissent faded sharply after 1920 with the secularisation of British society in the 20th century. The rise of the Labour Party reduced the Liberal Party strongholds into the nonconformist and remote "Celtic Fringe," where the party survived by an emphasis on localism and historic religious identity, thereby neutralising much of the class pressure on behalf of the Labour movement.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the Anglican church was a bastion of strength for the Conservative party. On the Irish issue, the Anglicans strongly supported unionism. Increasingly after 1850, the Roman Catholic element in England and Scotland was composed of recent immigrants from Ireland. They voted largely for the Irish Parliamentary Party, until its collapse in 1918.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Liberal_leaders_.281859.E2.80.931988.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_leaders_(1859\xe2\x80\x931988)">Liberal leaders (1859\xe2\x80\x931988)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
